SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/title|Patch Dynamics and Clonal Expansion of Reeds in the Letaba River, Kruger National Park
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/@id|1160116891007
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/@scope|document
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/individualName/surName|Kotschy
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/organizationName|University of the Witwatesrand
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/address/@scope|document
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/address/deliveryPoint|School of Animal Plant and Environmental Sciences
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/address/deliveryPoint|University of the Witwatesrand
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/abstract/para|A multiscale approach was used to study the dynamics of reed patches in a section of the Letaba River flowing through the Kruger National Park (KNP), South Africa's largest conservation area. The valley floor consists of a mosaic of patches of reeds, water, sand and rock. Reed vegetation change in space and time is closely linked to geomorphic change in the rivers of the KNP, because of reciprocal interactions between reeds, flow and sediment. Reeds are thus considered a potentially important species around which to formulate management goals for these rivers. A study of clonal growth characteristics at the scale of individual reedbeds was combined with a study of reed patch dynamics at the river reach scale. Mechanisms affecting clonal expansion across reedbed boundaries were combined with short-term patterns of reed persistence, loss and gain at the reach scale, and longer-term trends in reed cover, to produce a conceptual model of proposed relationships between flow conditions and reed cover in the Letaba River. Reed areal cover increased between 1988 and 1999. Despite this, reed persistence was low, and reeds were a dynamic element of the river landscape in the short term. Turnover of water and reed patches was high, within a matrix of sand. Complex patterns of reed gain and loss occurred at the boundaries of reed patches, with gain exceeding loss throughout the period of study. During a drier period (1988-1996), clonal expansion of patches and establishment of new patches contributed equally (50% each) to the increase in reed cover. During a wetter period (1996-1999), the relative contribution of clonal expansion increased to 90%. Reed persistence was higher in the wetter period due to less reed loss, and reed cover increased more rapidly. The relative proportions of reed, water and sand patches did not remain constant over 3 years or over 8 years in the alluvial section of the river studied. At the time and space scales of this study, therefore, the Letaba River did not display the kind of compositional stability described in other river systems. This points to the difficulty of applying equilibrium concepts of either geomorphology or vegetation dynamics to semi-arid rivers. A clear spatial patchiness was present in the distribution of sediment and nutrient resources on the macro-channel floor. Reed patches were associated with `current shadow' features, the characteristic raised elevation of the central, denser parts of reedbeds suggesting accumulation of sediment within reedbeds. Silt and clay particles accumulated preferentially within reedbeds. The results suggest that this accumulation was achieved primarily by selective retention of nes within reedbeds, and to a lesser extent by selective deposition of stones within reedbeds. Total carbon, nitrogen and phosphorus were all higher beneath reedbeds than in uncolonised sediment. The distributions of total N and total P were not correlated with that of total C, suggesting that nutrient accumulation does not simply reflect breakdown of reed organic matter. It was proposed that the nutrient accumulation recorded in this study was due primarily to interactions between reedbeds and flow, and to a lesser extent to the input of reed organic matter. The fact that surface resource distribution patterns did not always correspond to subsurface patterns illustrates the importance of viewing reed patches as three-dimensional entities. A high stem density and limited plasticity in spacer characteristics of Phragmites mauritianus in the Letaba River were consistent with a consolidation strategy of clonal growth. Physiological integration within within the clone was, however, limiteda notable departure from the consolidation strategy. While Phragmites mauritianus showed little evidence of plasticity in the horizontal spacer characteristics traditionally measured in studies of clonal growth patterns (internode length, branching pattern and bud activity), it was proposed that plasticity in rhizome characteristics related to regenerative capacity is important for reed persistence and clonal expansion in this habitat. The added vertical dimension of the rhizome system in species such as P. mauritianus limits the usefulness of horizontal spacer characteristics in explaining patterns of clonal expansion. Progressive accumulation of sediment above the rhizomes and upgrowth of reeds to the surface was proposed as a mechanism whereby the depth of the rhizome layer is increased, thus increasing the density of reed stems and enhancing the persistence of the reedbed. Rhizomes at reedbed boundaries did not show particular adaptations for rapid expansion into available space. Clonal expansion and consolidation of the space aroud established reedbeds was limited both by features of the rhizomes themselves (high proportion of dormant buds, low branching probability and shallow depth), and by external factors (flow disturbance). Management concerns over reed `encroachment' in the Letaba River appear unfounded. While rapid reed expansion may occur during favourable periods, conditions suitable for large increases in reed cover occur relatively infrequently and persist for short periods of time. It was proposed that wet periods with a high frequency of medium flows and no large floods are most likely to be associated with large increases in reed cover.

SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/title|Methods
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Carter and Rogers (1995) analysed changes in reed cover up until 1988. Aerial photographs
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|from 1988, 1996 and 1999 were used to extend their work. A Geographical
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Information System (GIS), PCI-ILWIS, was used to analyse the aerial photographs. The
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|GIS software made it possible to calculate not only changes in reed cover, but also changes
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|in the distribution of reeds within the macro-channel. It was thus possible to determine
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|the persistence of reedbeds and to assess the relative contributions of clonal expansion and
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|establishment of new patches to changes in reed cover. Spatial and temporal relationships
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|between reed patches, and water and sand patches, could also be investigated.
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Since the focus of this thesis is on the dynamics of reed patches, an alluvial section of the
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|river was chosen for study. Alluvial channel types have a smaller diversity of patches on
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|the macrochannel oor than other channel types, because of the absence of bedrock and
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|woody vegetation. Reed patches could also be distinguished relatively easily on the aerial
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|photographs. The 25 km llong study section lay between Mingerhout and Engelhard dams
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|(Figure 2.1). The work of Carter and Rogers (1995) showed that landscape state changes
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|taking place in this section reflect the changes taking place in the river as a whole, both in
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|overall pattern and in relative magnitude.
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Eight subsections were selected from the study section, each approximately 300 m in
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|length. These were chosen subjectively in order to cover both the length of the study
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|section and the range of histories of reed change (increasing reed cover, decreasing reed
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|cover, and no change). Four of the subsections included the study sites at which fieldwork
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|was done (Chapter 5).
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|The aerial photographs were digitized using a scanner, at a resolution of 300 dots per
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|inch (dpi), giving a pixel size of approximately 0.8 m
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|0.8 m on the ground. The three photographs from each site were then cross-georeferenced using features recognisable on all three photos (mainly trees). Pixels were classified as either reeds, water or sand using
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|unsupervised classification based on grayscale ranges. Woody vegetation could not be
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|uniquely identified on the basis of its grayscale range, and was omitted from the analysis.
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|This was not considered a problem as woody vegetation in this section of the Letaba occurs
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|almost exclusively as a riparian fringe on the macrochannel banks and does not form
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|part of the mosaic of patches on the macro-channel oor. Occasional `problem' patches
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|of woody vegetation were masked using the GIS software and thus ommitted from the
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|analysis. The accuracy of each classification was assessed visually from the photographs
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|and by comparing the maps produced by the GIS with reed distribution at the study sites.
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|The resulting maps of reed distribution for each year were then combined to produce
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|raster maps showing the location of patches (pixels) which had undergone one of nine
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|categories of change, as follows:
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|1) reeds-> reeds 4) sand-> reeds 7) water-> reeds
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|2) reeds-> sand 5) sand-> sand 8) water-> sand
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|3) reeds-> water 6) sand-> water 9) water-> water
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Two sets of these combined maps were produced, one for each of the two time periods
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|(1988 to 1996, and 1996 to 1999). This made it possible to compare changes taking place
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|over a longer and a shorter period (8 years and 3 years).
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|3.2.1 Short-term reed dynamics: changes in cover, persistence,
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/title|Short term reed dynamics: changes in cover, persistence, gain and loss
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|The GIS software was used to calculate the proportion of the macro-channel covered by
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|each of the nine categories of change for each period. Reed cover present at the beginning
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|of a period could then be calculated by combining categories 1, 2 and 3, and that present
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|at the end of a period by combining categories 1, 4 and 7. Changes in the proportion of
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|the macro-channel covered by water and sand were calculated in a similar way.
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Pattterns of reed persistence, appearance and disappearance were investigated by calculating
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|the frequencies of each type of change involving reeds (categories 1, 2, 3, 4 and 7)
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|as a percentage of the total changes involving reeds. These were compared between the
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|two time periods using two-sample t tests.
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Reed persistence is a measure of what proportion of reed patches were able to maintain
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|their position on the macro-channel floor, and thus, possibly, undergo clonal expansion. It
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|was calculated as the proportion of reeds which were not lost or gained during a period,
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|in other words, those that maintained their position in space (category 1). Reed turnover
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|was therefore defined as the reciprocal of persistence: the proportion of reed patches
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|which did not maintain their position in space. Reeds which were lost are given by the
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|relative frequencies of categories 2 and 3, while reeds which became established are given
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|by the frequencies of categories 4 and 7.
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Although this approach differs slightly from that of Arscott et al. (2002), where patch
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|turnover was calculated as the proportional area of new patches present at the end of
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|each time period, it allows patterns of reed loss and reed gain to be distinguished, and
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|therefore allows for a more detailed analysis of reed dynamics. The term turnover, as
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|used by Arscott et al. (2002), is misleading in the sense that it suggests complete removal
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|and re-establishment of patches, whereas in fact it simply indicates the extent of new
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|reed area, which may have arisen either by establishment of new patches or by clonal
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|expansion of existing patches. Patch turnover was, however, also calculated as described
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|above, to allow the results of this study to be compared with those of Arscott et al. (2002),
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|the only other known study of patch turnover in a disturbance-driven river system.
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|It was hypothesised that high reed persistence increases the potential for clonal expansion,
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|and thus produces a greater increase in cover. This hypothesis was tested by relating
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|percentage change in reed cover in each subsection to reed persistence using regression
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|models.
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|The response variable for regression models was always plotted against the fitted values
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|to allow visual inspection of the fit of the model. The model fit was assessed further using
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|the following diagnostic plots:
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Residuals vs fitted values, useful for identifying outliers and visualising structure in
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|the residuals
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Normal quantile plot of residuals, to test the assumption that the model's errors are
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|normally distributed
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Scale-location plot, for measuring spread and identifying pattern in the residuals
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Cook's distance plot, a measure of the inuence of individual observations on the
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|regression coefficients
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|To determine whether the fate of reed patches depends on their association with other
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|patch types, the probabilities of association between reeds and all the other categories of
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|change were calculated for each river section. This was done by calculating, for each type
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|of reed pixel, the number of neighbouring pixels of each of the 9 categories of change.
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Reed pixels included reeds which remained (cagegory 1); reeds which were lost (categories
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|2 and 3); and new reeds which became established (categories 4 and 7). While
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|pixels of a particular type are most likely to be surrounded by pixels of the same type, it is
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|the relationships with neighbouring pixels of different types that can provide insight into
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|the spatial associations between changes involving reeds, and those involving water and
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|sand.
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Spatial associations between persistent reed patches and areas of reed loss were further
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|quantified by estimating from the maps the proportion of patches of loss which lay within
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|a 5 m radius of persistent reed patches. This made it possible to estimate what proportion
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|of patches were removed completely, and what proportion merely became smaller.
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/title|Clonal expansion vs establishment of new reed patches
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Patches of new reeds which are close to persistent reed patches are most probably a result
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|of clonal expansion of the persistent patches, whereas those which are far from persistent
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|patches must have established from seed or clonal progagules. The relative contribution
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|of these two types of reed gain was determined by estimating the proportion of new reed
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|patches found within a 5m radius of persistent patches, as described above for reed loss.
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Edge:area ratios were also calculated for all types of patches and compared between time
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|periods using two-sample t tests. A decrease in this ratio indicates that patches have
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|become more consolidated, suggesting clonal expansion in the case of reed patches. An
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|increase in edge:area ratio indicates that patches have become smaller and more fragmented.
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Thus it was possible to determine whether a particular type of change occurred
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|in large contiguous areas or rather in small discontinuous patches.
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Due to computational constraints, it was not possible to convert the raster maps of whole
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|river subsections into vector format, necessary for calculating edge:area ratios. Instead,
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|two randomly selected smaller areas (approximately 100 m in length) from each subsection
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|map were converted into vector format and used to calculate edge:area ratios.
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|The submaps converted into vector format for the calculation of edge:area ratios were also
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|used to calculate the relative lengths of vegetated and unvegetated channel edge. The fact
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|that new areas of water could be distinguished from older, more persistent ones made it
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|possible to estimate the time required for channel edges to become colonised by reeds.
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/title|Relationships between reed dynamics and geomorphic change
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Given the highly dynamic nature of the patch mosaic in semi-arid rivers generally, reed
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|patch dynamics is likely to be related to geomorphic change, which may be quantified by
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|the dynamics of water and sand patches. Relationships between reed patch dynamics and
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|geomorphic change were explored by:
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Determining probabilities of association between reed patches, and water and sand
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|patches with different histories (neighbour analysis)
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Comparing the persistence and turnover of reed and water patches in each period
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Relating reed cover and persistence to water cover and persistence using regression
SANPARKS|00293-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|models

SANPARKS|00324-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|The census methods and associated conditions are described in the annual reports, and for the EAS surveys by Viljoen and Retief (Koedoe No. 37, pp. 149-157, 1994). In summary they were conducted by fixed-wing aircraft flying parallel strips, generally 800 m apart, aiming at complete ground coverage. However, all regions of the Kruger Park were fully covered only over 1980-1993. When coverage was incomplete, the park total for each species was projected from the trend in the regions that were covered. Count totals for the period earlier than 1977 were adjusted for the lower visibility from the air of certain species using differential ground counts. The park perimeter was entirely fenced until 1993, when sections of the western boundary fence adjoining private nature reserves were removed. Count totals have been sub-divided among four regions: South = between Crocodile and Sabi Rivers; Central = between Sabi and Olifants Rivers; North = between Olifants and Letaba Rivers; Far North = north of Letaba River, excluding the section north of Punda Maria that was covered irregularly

SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/title|Salvadora australis as an ecosystem engineer of sodic sites in the northern Kruger National Park
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/@id|1154941888281
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/@scope|document
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/individualName/surName|Teren
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/organizationName|Gabriella
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/address/@scope|document
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/address/deliveryPoint|School of Animal Plant and Environmental Sciences, University of the Witwatesrand
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/electronicMailAddress|teren@gecko.biol.wits.ac.za
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/abstract/para|The Phugwane River in the northern Kruger National Park is flanked by bare sodic sites, of which Salvadora angustifolia Turril var. australis (Schweik.) Verdoon is the dominant woody species which grows in characteristic islands associated with undercanopy vegetation. Little vegetation grows between these islands, and this has led to the hypothesis that Salvadora australis is an ecosystem engineer of sodic sites, modulating soil properties which allows other vegetation to grow. A differential browsing response on these trees across sites also occurs. An investigation was undertaken to assess differences in physical and chemical properties of the soil under the canopy of trees and away from trees. Foliar chemistry, browsing response and tree physiognomy across sites was also assessed. Soil under trees showed significantly decreased sodicity, concentrations of extractable sodium and magnesium and increased concentrations of calcium, C: N ratio and proportions of larger soil particles. Trees also increase in size and possibly age towards the riparian and upland boundaries of sites. Size was not correlated to a salinity gradient in the soil under trees or in the leaves of trees. This species has been shown to be a halophyte that accumulates sodium in the leaves at increasing concentrations with higher external salinity concentrations. It also performs selective portioning of ions as a tolerance strategy where old leaves have higher sodium concentrations and lower potassium concentrations than younger leaves. Browsing preference was not correlated with higher sodium concentrations of trees or leaves. By inferring mechanisms of solute and water movement, this species has been shown to be an ecosystem engineer of the soil properties underneath it, which results in a better growing environment for other plants. This increases habitat heterogeneity across sites and species richness under canopies of trees. The increased biodiversity this species engineers has important managerial implications for Kruger National Park and demonstrates the importance of ecosystem engineers in semi-arid savanna ecosystems.

SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/title|Soil and Leaf Sample Collection
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Ten trees were sampled at each site along a transect running the length of the bare patch perpendicular to the river to establish a gradient across the site to the river. The boundaries of the bare patch were characterised by vegetation and soil change from bare hard crusty soils to darker, more vegetated looser soils. The transect started from the riparian boundary of the bare area (characterised by occurrence of Colophospermum mopane and riparian vegetation) to the upland boundary (characterised by increasing frequency of C. mopane trees and decreasing frequency of S. australis.
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Two soil cores were dug with an auger for each tree, with one at the base of the tree under the canopy and the other in bare soils, 1.5m from the edge of the canopy. Soil samples were taken every 20cm till one metre deep (0-20cm; 20-40cm; 40-60cm; 60-80cm; 80-100cm) with a total of 10 samples per tree. Soil was placed in brown paper bags, sifted through a 2mm sieve to mix the sample, and remove roots and gravel and dried at 65°C. The auger holes were back-filled with surrounding soil after the samples were removed.
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Leaf samples were taken as a bulked sample from the whole canopy for each tree. This was then separated into young and old leaves, based on position on the stem and size of the leaf. The leaves were then dipped in deionized water to remove dust on the surface. Care was taken to avoid salt contamination by the use of latex gloves and minimal handling of leaves. The leaves were then dried at 55 °C until dry and easily broken.
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Three additional trees of different size classes were sampled for texture analysis: 1 small, heavily browsed shrub (height =39cm), a medium shrub with a definite browse line (height =210cm), and a large tree that shows evidence of relatively little browsing (height =340cm). A trench was dug from the base of the tree to ~1.5m away from the edge of the canopy.
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Soil samples were taken in horizons to a depth of 60cm, at intervals every 50cm along the trench for the small and medium trees and every 100cm for the large tree. Soil was investigated in the field for texture, colour, frequency of roots and occurrence of soil biota.
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|For these samples, texture analysis was performed in the laboratory by hydrometer method. This method uses sodium hexametamosphate to separate particles of different sizes from each other in a soil solution. The solution thoroughly mixed with an electrical stirrer and then left to settle for different periods when readings on the hydrometer are taken (40 seconds for %silt (20-50µm); 4 minutes for %silt (<2-20µm) and 2 hours for % clay (<2 µm)). % sand (50-2000µm) was calculated as the remaining percent. Calculations are corrected for temperature and moisture of the soil. pH and electroconductivity were also measured using hand-held field pH and E.C. meters in a 20g soil: 50ml deionised water solution (Anderson and Ingram, 1996).
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Soil was sent to the Agricultural Research Council where it was analysed for total nitrogen by automated Kjedahl method, and carbon by modified Walkley-Black method.
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/title|Tree physiognomy
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Tree physiognomy data were collected for each tree:
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|- tree height
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|- canopy diameter at the longest length
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|- Average stem circumference
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|- Browse height if actively browsed tree
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Tree height was measured using a tape measure and/or ranging rod and taken from the base of the tree. Average stem circumference was determined from individual stem circumferences measured above the basal swelling of the tree. Browse height was taken as the height of very short trees that are browsed low, and from the ground to the bottom of a browse line on other trees. Trees that showed no browse line were given a score of 0.
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|The distance from each tree to the centre of the transect and sodic site was also estimated using a hand-held Garmin G.P.S. (with accuracy of ~1m).
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/title|Undercanopy Species Composition
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Undercanopy woody and grass species were collected for each tree to asses species richness and composition. The species were pressed and identified in the Wits herbarium. Some specimens could not be conclusively identified as they were collected in the dry season and exhibited few taxonomic features.
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/title|Large Herbivore Exclosure Experiment
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|An exclosure experiment was set up in order to determine if herbivores are selecting for leaves with particular nutrient concentrations such as higher sodium on individual trees. Actively browsed trees from each site along the transect were selected with 2 near the outer edge of the site and one in the centre (n =9). Leaves were removed from branches that showed evidence of herbivory (leaves removed or ends of stems nipped) representing leaves that had not been selected by herbivores (pre-exclosure sample). The branches from which, these leaves had been removed, were marked with pink plastic ribbon. An exclosure was then constructed of 5mm green wire mesh, metal fence droppers and cable ties or coated flexible wire. For very small bushes, the entire bush was fenced off using the fencing droppers hammered into the ground (see figure 4 in the appendix). Individual browsed branches on larger trees were exclosed. Here the mesh was wrapped around the branch to bag the branch and tied using cable ties and wire (see figure 5 in the appendix). Where the wire bag was hard to see, white latex gloves were tied to it to prevent danger to any animals. Care was taken on all exclosures to ensure that the wire did not touch the growing shoots.
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Nine weeks later I sampled leaves that had grown on the marked branches subsequent to the exclosure being erected (post-exclosure sample) as leaves that would have been browsed had herbivores not been prevented from feeding. Old leaves were also collected from within the exclosure to compare to the new post-exclosure leaves. Some exclosures were damaged by elephants and on one (44/5), the exclosure was removed and the tree appeared to have been subsequently browsed, therefore no sample was taken. Some branches also did not have sufficient leaf growth to sample for old leaves (68/5; 44/8). The exclosures were then dismantled and removed. Leaf samples were dried and milled for further analysis.
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/title|Soil and Leaf Chemistry Determination
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Electrical conductivity and pH measurements were taken in the laboratory. The soil was sent to the Agricultural Research Council (ARC) in Pretoria for cation and SAR analysis. Soil samples from the first 8 trees from the upland side of site 83 were analysed for SAR as they showed high E.C. values in comparison with other trees. SAR was determined from a saturated paste extract using the Dumas method. The same soil samples from site 83 and samples from the exclosure trees from each site were analysed for selected extractable cations (Na+; Ca2+; Mg2+) and cation exchange capacity (CEC). Extractable cation analysis was by ammonium acetate extraction method (Anderson and Ingram, 1996), and determined by atomic absorption spectroscopy.
SANPARKS|00341-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|The dried leaf samples were milled to 0.5mm using a plant mill and sent to the Agricultural Research Council for selected nutrient analysis. The samples were analysed for Na+; Ca2+; Mg2+; K+; P using a mixed acid digestion and determined on an atomic absorption spectrophotometer.

SANPARKS|00346-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/abstract/para|The issue of waterholes is a critical one for Kruger Park management. Though initially constructed to boost numbers of antelope and other animal species, many studies have demonstrated the negative effects of artificial waterholes, which create a zone of decreased biodiversity known as a piosphere (Gaylard et al.). One year prior to our study, students from the Organization of Tropical Studies did a rodent trapping study to examine the effects of artificial waterholes on rodent diversity by comparing rodent communities at an open waterhole and one that had been inactive for two years. We used similar live-trapping methods to examine the effects of one more year of inactivity at the closed waterhole, as well as the effects of a burn that had occurred at the closed waterhole within the past year. Similar to the previous study, we found significantly greater rodent and grass diversity at the closed waterhole. The overall rodent abundance was lower for our study than the previous study, likely due to the decreased grass cover at both sites. We also found dramatically increased numbers of the bushveld gerbil, Tatera leucogaster, at the burned site, supporting the notion that burning has an effect on rodent species composition.
SANPARKS|00346-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/keywordSet/keyword|Mastomys, disturbance, fire, rodents, Sherman traps, Tatera, waterholes
SANPARKS|00346-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/keywordSet/keyword|SANParks, South Africa
SANPARKS|00346-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/keywordSet/keyword|Kruger National Park, South Africa

SANPARKS|00346-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/title|Live trapping method
SANPARKS|00346-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Live trapping study. In order to compare our trapping data to those from the previous year, we used the same open and closed waterholes as our study sites (open: 22°42’18&quot; S, 31°09’43&quot; E; closed: 22°49’40&quot; S, 31°14’21&quot; E, see Figure 1). Live trapping was conducted over 4 days (April 9-12, 2005). Two trapping grids were laid out on either side of the water troughs. We attempted to replicate the orientation and exact location of the grids in last year’s study, which had been placed approximately 20m from the waterholes to avoid severe overgrazing and trampling. Each grid included 30 traps (5 × 6) spaced 10 m apart. Within each line of traps we used two long Sherman traps (8 × 9.5 × 30 cm) and four short (8 × 9 × 23 cm). The distribution of the long traps within each row was randomized. To prevent any animals from experiencing hypothermia due to the damp conditions at the beginning of the study, pieces of synthetic stuffing were placed in each trap to absorb water and provide additional insulation. Traps were baited with seed each evening and were checked each morning. The traps were left open throughout the day and night. We identified each trapped individual with a unique marking using black hair dye. We used a single numbering system for both grids at each site in case individuals crossed between the grids. The mass, sex, approximate age class and species was recorded for each individual.
SANPARKS|00346-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/title|vegetation survey
SANPARKS|00346-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|. During our initial assessment of the study sites, it was noted that the vegetation was considerably sparser and drier than the previous year, and that the closed waterhole site had burned recently (see Figure 2). We assessed the vegetation at each grid along 100 m transects, which were laid out from the periphery of the water trough through the center of the grid. At 10 m intervals we estimated visibility perpendicular to the transect at heights of 10 and 50 cm. This was done by measuring the distance at which &gt;50 percent of an object was obscured when viewed from each of the two heights. We used these heights as a representation of the effects of grass cover on rodent visibility, assuming that visibility plays a role in habitat preference. Percent grass cover was estimated every ten meters within a 50 cm × 50 cm quadrat. We measured grass species composition by identifying the grass species closest to the transect line every 2 m along the transect.
SANPARKS|00346-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/title|Startistical analyses
SANPARKS|00346-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|We used Microsoft Excel to compile our data and Stats Alive to perform ratio analyses to determine whether there was a significantly higher trap success rate in long versus short traps or any significant edge effect. All other statistical analyses were performed using Primer and Statistica. Using Primer, we calculated species similarity values for the vegetation transects and rodent trapping gridsand we calculated the Simpson’s diversity index for the grass community at each grid. We used Simpson’s measure of alpha diversity because it produces a weighted species richness measure using the probability of selecting two individuals of the same species at random from the same community. We used Statistica to perform t-tests to look for significant differences between the open and closed transects in terms of the percent grass cover and visibility.
SANPARKS|00346-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|We used the Peterson method to estimate the population of rodents at each site, which uses the unbiased estimator equation: N = (((M+1)(C+1))/(R+1)) -1, where M = number of individuals marked in the first sample, C = total number of individuals captured in the second sample and R = number of individuals in second sample that are marked (Krebs 1999). We combined the capture numbers for both grids within each site to avoid overestimating the population since there had been crossover of individuals between grids.

SANPARKS|00349-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/abstract/para|In this study, we examined whether leaf presence affected ant abundances on Acacia grandicornuta, and how the presence of scale insects on Acacia robusta affected ant species abundance. In order to asses the benefits accrued by the plants, we also tested attack times of three genera of ants following a disturbance to the host tree. It was determined that both ant species richness and concentration were significantly greater on A. grandicornuta containing leaves. This result suggests that the presence of leaves drives the mutualism between A. grandicornuta and ants, and when leaves are not present, ant concentrations decrease. We also determined that Black Sugar Ants (Lepisiota capensis) exist in greater abundance on A. robusta when scale insects are present, and this populace of L. capensis correlates to a reduction in the abundance of other ant species. Finally, our results show that L. capensis attack significantly quicker than other ant genera following a disturbance to the host tree. This demonstrates the ability of L. capensis to colonize and dominate a host tree, causing a significant decrease in the abundance of other ant genera. These results convey that a facultative mutualism exists among A. robusta, L. capensis, and scale insects; L. capensis provides protection for both A. robusta and scale insects, while scale insects offer nutritional benefits to the resident ants.

SANPARKS|00349-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/title|Site selection and Field sampling
SANPARKS|00349-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|This study was conducted in and around the Skukuza rest camp of Kruger National Park, Mpumalanga province, South Africa (24°98” S 31°60” E). We conducted and performed all field work for our study and subsequent experiment over the course of a three day period from October 10, 2005 to October 12, 2005, near the end of the dry season.
SANPARKS|00349-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Our study species were Acacia grandicornuta and Acacia robusta. A. grandicornuta is a medium-sized tree with deep fissured bark and paired, short thorns, and is often found on either sodic or clay soils throughout the north-eastern region of South Africa. A. grandicornuta is tolerant of dry conditions and is normally leafless from August to October. A. robusta is a single-stemmed tree with paired, straight, modified stipules as thorns and an annual leaf presence. A. robusta tends to grow throughout the north-eastern region of South Africa, typically near a water source and it rarely occurs in homogeneous stands. Neither A. grandicornuta nor A. robusta are known to produce extra floral nectaries, food bodies or beltian bodies (Smit 1999).
SANPARKS|00349-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|We examined 26 Acacia grandicornuta individuals—12 possessing leaves and 14 lacking leaves—all located in and around the staff village of the Skukuza main camp, primarily near the golf course. We chose to observe A. grandicornuta in order to compare the status of this facultative ant-plant mutualism when leaves were present and absent. This was possible as A. grandicornuta loses its leaves from late August to early October, thus yielding a sufficient abundance of individuals with and without leaves. We then examined 14 Acacia robusta individuals—all of which possessed leaves.
SANPARKS|00349-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|For each A. grandicornuta and A. robusta individual encountered, we recorded a number of observations: the average abundance of ants, ant species composition, the presence or absence of scale insects, and the degree of defensive behavior of the ants on each tree. The abundance of ants on each tree was obtained by selecting two .25m by .5m sections, estimating the number of ants per section, and averaging the two abundance measures. We identified our ant samples down to genus, and in one instance down to species. Following our initial study, we conducted a subsequent experiment in which we generated an artificial disturbance. To mimic natural herbivory processes we scratched the trunk of the tree while simultaneously shaking a branch.
SANPARKS|00349-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Analysis
SANPARKS|00349-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Mann-Whitney U-tests were performed to test for significant differences in ant abundance dependent on leaf presence and scale insect presence in A. grandicornuta and A. robusta respectively. A Kruskal-Wallis test was conducted to determine differences in attack time among three ant genera.

SANPARKS|00355-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/title|Vegetation Survey
SANPARKS|00355-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|As the EAS vegetation assessment and veld condition assessment surveys only measure the herbaceous vegetation at a very coarse level, I conducted a separate vegetation survey to answer specific questions. The two main objectives of this vegetation survey were: 1) to compare vegetation inside and outside an enclosure to determine the general condition of the herbaceous layer in the roan’s range, and 2) to determine the effects that the artificial waterholes have had on the composition and abundance in the herbaceous layer. The vegetation survey was conducted in January 1994.
SANPARKS|00355-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/title|Comparison of vegetation inside and outside the enclosure
SANPARKS|00355-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|The enclosure used for this survey is known as Nwashitsumbe and is situated in the northeast of the roan’s range (refer to Figure 2.1). It is a square, 256 ha. Enclosure and has been protected from large numbers of grazers since 1970, except for 20 roan between 1970 and 1978, and 40 Lichtenstein’s hartebeest between 1991 and the time of the study. The eastern fence of the enclosure runs through an alluvial grassland, while the remaining three sides of the enclosure run through non-alluvial grassland.
SANPARKS|00355-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Specific attributes that were measured included: composition, abundance, cover and height of the herbaceous layer. Sampling sites were located on both the outside and the inside of the four fence lines of the enclosure. Sampling sites were positioned ten meters from the fence as the Lichtensstein’s hartebeest present at the time of the study may have concentrated in these areas.
SANPARKS|00355-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|At each sampling site a point quadrate survey of 225 random points (by means of a step-point method) was conducted to determine the botanical composition of the herbaceous layer. Tree rows of 75 points were located on each side of the enclosure fence and the nearest grass species to each point and the grass species height was recorded (see Figure 2.3 for schematic representation). If there was not an herbaceous species within the 25 cm of the point this was recorded as &quot;bare ground&quot;. Cover of the herbaceous layer was determined using a quadrate method in which and 8-point scale with uneven class intervals was used, as described by Anderson and Walker (1974). Walker 1976) demonstrated that this scale was the most accurate and repeatable method for measuring herbaceous cover. The scale is as follows:

SANPARKS|00355-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|The above mentioned were chosen as they are the same methods used during the veld condition assessment surveys (Trollope et al, 1989) and so enabled comparison of results.
SANPARKS|00355-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Vegetation surrounding artificial waterholes
SANPARKS|00355-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|As well sampling inside and outside the enclosure, there waterholes in the Vlakteplaas east compartment were sampled at increasing distance from the water to determine the effect these waterholes have had on the composition and abundance of species in the herbaceous layer. The three waterholes included Hlamalala-south, Papanyane and Boyela-north as they all occurred in vleis. Roan prefers vlei (alluvial) grasslands (Heitkoning, 1993, Joubert, 1976), so only waterholes occurring in vleis were examined. This allowed of the effects of waterholes on the herbaceous layer in both the vleis and the grassland.
SANPARKS|00355-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Eight transects were placed at each waterhole, two replicate transects in each of the four major directions leading away from the waterhole. As all three waterholes sampled occurred within vleis, four transects occurred within the vlei (two replicate transects in opposite directions from the waterhole), while the remaining four transects occurred in the grassland (two replicate transects in opposite directions from the waterhole) (see Figure 2.4 from schematic representation). Replicate transects were placed 20 meters apart. The minimum length of a transect was 150 metres, although transects were continued until the proportion of increaser II grass species dropped below 50%. This figure was used at it was thought that the natural proportion of increaser II grass species in this area was somewhere between 40 and 50% (Abates, pers.comm). The grass species were classified according Trollope (1990) with respect to their response to different grazing intensities (see Appendix 1).
SANPARKS|00355-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|In addition to the three waterholes sampled, two vlei grassland which did not contain waterholes were located and their composition, abundance, height and cover were determined for composition with the sampled vleis that did contain waterholes. This was to determine whether there were differences between vleis which contained waterholes with those that did not. The vlei system were Hlamala and Papanyane. The sampling methods were identical to those used to sample Nwashitsumbe enclosure, except that transects were extended beyond 150 metres until the proportion of increaser II grass species dropped below 50%. A point-step method was used where points were located every two metres on the transect. At each point the nearest grass species was identified and its height measured, while the herbaceous cover was measured using a 1X1 meter quadrant at every third point along the transect (i.e. every six metres).
SANPARKS|00355-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Vegetation survey
SANPARKS|00355-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Where more detail was needed to supplement the EAS vegetation assessment and veld condition assessment survey, a vegetation survey was conducted. This compared the grassland inside and outside the Nwashitsumbe enclosure which is 256 ha. In area and has been protected from large numbers of grazers since 1970, except for 20 roan in the 1970’s and 40 Lichtentein’s hartebeest since 1991. Specific attributes that were measured included composition, abundance, cover, and height of the herbaceous layer, using a point-step method. Composition of the herbaceous layer was determined according to the dry weight rank method described by Mannetje and Haydock (1963), while abundance, cover and height was determined using a quadrant method described by Walker (1976).
SANPARKS|00355-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|On each side of the enclosure three 150 m transects were run (i.e. 4 * 3 transects) along which the grass species was identified and its height measured every 2 meters and the basal cover was measured every 6 meters. Transects were replaced mid way down the enclosures fence line, and care was taken that they were not placed near the fence or drinking troughs as the animals have concentrated in these areas(The transects were placed next to the road, on the bottom side)
SANPARKS|00355-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|As well as sampling inside and outside the enclosure, three waterholes in the Vlakteplaas east compartment were sampled at increasing distance from the water to determine the effect these waterholes have had on the composition and abundance of species in the herbaceous layer. The three waterholes sampled included Hlamalala-south, Papayane and Boyela-north. As roan prefers vlei (alluvial) grasslands, only waterholes occurring in vlei's were examined. This allowed comparison of the effects of waterholes on the herbaceous layer between the vlei’s and the grassland. Eight transects were run at each waterhole, four into the vlei (two replicate transects on each side of the waterhole). Replicate transects were placed 20 meters apart. The minimum length of a transect was 150 meters, although transects were run until the proportion of increaser II grass species dropped to approximately 50%. There are 44 waterholes in this compartment, so the three waterholes were chosen so that a large proportion of the roan range was represented. This was to ensure that the observed vegetation changes have occurred at waterholes in all the areas in which roan occur. Two vlei grassland which did not contain waterholes were located and their composition, abundance, height and cover were determined for comparison with the sampled vleis that did contain waterholes. The vlei system sampled were Hlamalala and Papanyane. This work was conducted during December 1993 and January 1994.

SANPARKS|00359-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/abstract/para|PROJECT GOALS & OBJECTIVES In my initial project registration, I outlined several main objectives as follows: Short-term To quantify demographic data collected within the Klaserie Private Nature Reserve (KPNR) by Christianne Knechtel and create a life-history model with climate sensitive parameters. To create a GIS of movement data collected by C. Knechtel to quantify daily movement rates, direction, route and range; to analyze and compare this data with ongoing data collection in the Satara region of KNP (P.Cross). To create comparable databases of climatic data for both KPNR and KNP; monthly rainfall and daily temperature data will be used. To compile databases of GIS information regarding habitat (satellite imagery, vegetation analyses, soil type, slope and aspect, hydrology) for both KPNR and KNP. Long-term To create a GIS model of buffalo response to climate and habitat, incorporating demographically driven age-structured disease transmission. Assess management strategies (vaccination, culling, exclusion zones) within this GIS modeled environment of Kruger National Park. To provide Kruger National Park with information and data regarding modeled strategies, habitat data and crucial demographic data for African Buffalo. In pursuing these goals and objectives, I sought to complement ongoing monitoring of bovine tuberculosis in the Satara region with studies of buffalo ranging and demography. Several studies of buffalo ranging, habitat selection and demography were undertaken with the overarching goal of describing the relationship between buffalo and their resources in a spatially explicit and temporally dynamic way.

SANPARKS|00360-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/abstract/para|Arundo donax is an invasive weed which was recently discovered on the Sabie River in Kruger National Park and like many invasives it is thought to reduce biodiversity in its invaded environment. In this experiment we looked at the effects of the invasive weed on insect biodiversity in a riparian environment. We compared the species richness of A. donax to the native reed, Phragmites mauritianus and to a mixed vegetation environment. We found that A. donax did not significantly decrease the number of individuals or number of species found in pitfall traps. Biological diversity indices showed, however, that there was in fact a higher diversity in the A. donax insect populations. We also took measurements of the insect diversity of the three vegetation types by beating the reeds and analyzing the insects that fell out. We found a lower species richness in A. donax than in P. mauritianus. This can be explained by the fact that one species of ant is dominating P. mauritianus on the ground but dominating A. donax in the upper layers of the reed. This, in turn, says that the dominating species is pushing out other species accounting for a lowered diversity that might otherwise be found. In our study, A. donax did not significantly affect the biodiversity of insects overall found on P mauritianus within a riparian ecosystem.

SANPARKS|00360-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/title|methods
SANPARKS|00360-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|In order to determine the effects of invasion by A. donax on insect biodiversity, we conducted a study on the Sabie River (24. 97978 0S, 31. 48186 0 E) in Kruger National Park, Mpumalanga Province, South Africa, on 10 October 2005. We laid pitfall traps to assess insect abundance within three different sites, viz. stands of the native reed, Phragmites mauritianus, stands of the invasive reed Arundo donax as well as mixed stands of the two reed types. We chose two sites of the native, P. mauritianus, big enough to sufficiently cover a 2m by 2m matrix of pitfall traps, two sites of the invasive reed, A. donax and two sites of mixed vegetation. 2 different sized pitfall traps were used- the larger pitfall traps were 10 cm in diameter and 10 cm in depth, and the smaller traps were 4 cm in diameter and 5 cm deep. At each site, we laid nine pitfall traps in a 2m by 2m matrix, with alternating sized traps 1m apart. (Figure 1) Each vegetation type had one of each matrix type shown in Figure 1 A and B. This gave us 4 small and 5 big traps at one site and 5 small and 4 big at the other site for each vegetation type. Once all the traps were laid, we filled them each ¼ of the way full with anti-freeze to kill any insects that fell into the trap that could possibly climb or fly out.
SANPARKS|00360-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|Insects were collected 2 days later from the traps, and were identified by morphospecies back at the laboratory. On the same day that the pitfall traps were cleared, in order to collect another type of data, we collected insects from all 3 vegetation types by beating the vegetation. We placed a round tray with a 50cm diameter on the ground and repeatedly beat the vegetation with a 1 meter stick for 20 seconds. We then placed all the insects that fell out of the reeds onto the tray into vials and labelled them. This was repeated 10 times at each vegetation type (A. donax, mixed vegetation and P.mauritianus). These individuals were also taken back to the lab and identified to morphospecies.
SANPARKS|00360-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/methods/methodStep/description/section/para|In order to determine whether the diversity of insects was significantly different in the three sites, data for the number of individuals per trap, and the number of species per trap were analysed using Kruskal-Wallis, multiple comparison of means tests. (Statistica version. 6) When certain values were not considered significant we then did a post-hoc test to determine which, if any, of the relationships were significant. Similarities or differences in the insect communities were then determined using Primer to do a Multi Dimensional Scaling (MDS) plot, a similarity matrix and a biodiversity index for the number of individuals and species we collected in each trap. Next, we used Stastica again to do a Kruskal-Wallis test on the number of individuals collected per beating and the number of species per beating. Primer was then used to calculate a similarity matrix and a biodiversity index for the beating samples

SANPARKS|00382-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/title|Patch Dynamics and Clonal Expansion of Reeds in the Letaba River, Kruger National Park
SANPARKS|00382-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/@id|1160116891007
SANPARKS|00382-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/@scope|document
SANPARKS|00382-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/individualName/surName|Kotschy
SANPARKS|00382-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/organizationName|University of the Witwatesrand
SANPARKS|00382-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/address/@scope|document
SANPARKS|00382-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/address/deliveryPoint|School of Animal Plant and Environmental Sciences
SANPARKS|00382-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/creator/address/deliveryPoint|University of the Witwatesrand
SANPARKS|00382-metadata.xml|/ns0:eml/dataset/abstract/para|A multiscale approach was used to study the dynamics of reed patches in a section of the Letaba River flowing through the Kruger National Park (KNP), South Africa's largest conservation area. The valley floor consists of a mosaic of patches of reeds, water, sand and rock. Reed vegetation change in space and time is closely linked to geomorphic change in the rivers of the KNP, because of reciprocal interactions between reeds, flow and sediment. Reeds are thus considered a potentially important species around which to formulate management goals for these rivers. A study of clonal growth characteristics at the scale of individual reedbeds was combined with a study of reed patch dynamics at the river reach scale. Mechanisms affecting clonal expansion across reedbed boundaries were combined with short-term patterns of reed persistence, loss and gain at the reach scale, and longer-term trends in reed cover, to produce a conceptual model of proposed relationships between flow conditions and reed cover in the Letaba River. Reed areal cover increased between 1988 and 1999. Despite this, reed persistence was low, and reeds were a dynamic element of the river landscape in the short term. Turnover of water and reed patches was high, within a matrix of sand. Complex patterns of reed gain and loss occurred at the boundaries of reed patches, with gain exceeding loss throughout the period of study. During a drier period (1988-1996), clonal expansion of patches and establishment of new patches contributed equally (50% each) to the increase in reed cover. During a wetter period (1996-1999), the relative contribution of clonal expansion increased to 90%. Reed persistence was higher in the wetter period due to less reed loss, and reed cover increased more rapidly. The relative proportions of reed, water and sand patches did not remain constant over 3 years or over 8 years in the alluvial section of the river studied. At the time and space scales of this study, therefore, the Letaba River did not display the kind of compositional stability described in other river systems. This points to the difficulty of applying equilibrium concepts of either geomorphology or vegetation dynamics to semi-arid rivers. A clear spatial patchiness was present in the distribution of sediment and nutrient resources on the macro-channel floor. Reed patches were associated with `current shadow' features, the characteristic raised elevation of the central, denser parts of reedbeds suggesting accumulation of sediment within reedbeds. Silt and clay particles accumulated preferentially within reedbeds. The results suggest that this accumulation was achieved primarily by selective retention of nes within reedbeds, and to a lesser extent by selective deposition of stones within reedbeds. Total carbon, nitrogen and phosphorus were all higher beneath reedbeds than in uncolonised sediment. The distributions of total N and total P were not correlated with that of total C, suggesting that nutrient accumulation does not simply reflect breakdown of reed organic matter. It was proposed that the nutrient accumulation recorded in this study was due primarily to interactions between reedbeds and flow, and to a lesser extent to the input of reed organic matter. The fact that surface resource distribution patterns did not always correspond to subsurface patterns illustrates the importance of viewing reed patches as three-dimensional entities. A high stem density and limited plasticity in spacer characteristics of Phragmites mauritianus in the Letaba River were consistent with a consolidation strategy of clonal growth. Physiological integration within within the clone was, however, limiteda notable departure from the consolidation strategy. While Phragmites mauritianus showed little evidence of plasticity in the horizontal spacer characteristics traditionally measured in studies of clonal growth patterns (internode length, branching pattern and bud activity), it was proposed that plasticity in rhizome characteristics related to regenerative capacity is important for reed persistence and clonal expansion in this habitat. The added vertical dimension of the rhizome system in species such as P. mauritianus limits the usefulness of horizontal spacer characteristics in explaining patterns of clonal expansion. Progressive accumulation of sediment above the rhizomes and upgrowth of reeds to the surface was proposed as a mechanism whereby the depth of the rhizome layer is increased, thus increasing the density of reed stems and enhancing the persistence of the reedbed. Rhizomes at reedbed boundaries did not show particular adaptations for rapid expansion into available space. Clonal expansion and consolidation of the space aroud established reedbeds was limited both by features of the rhizomes themselves (high proportion of dormant buds, low branching probability and shallow depth), and by external factors (flow disturbance). Management concerns over reed `encroachment' in the Letaba River appear unfounded. While rapid reed expansion may occur during favourable periods, conditions suitable for large increases in reed cover occur relatively infrequently and persist for short periods of time. It was proposed that wet periods with a high frequency of medium flows and no large floods are most likely to be associated with large increases in reed cover.
